		A \textit{foliated atlas} of codimension $q$ and class $C^r$ on the $n$-manifold $M$ is a $C^r$-atlas $\mathfrak{A}\bydef\left\{\sU_\a \right\}_{\a \in \mathscr A}$  of foliated charts of codimension $q$ which are \textit{coherently foliated} in the sense that, whenever $P$ and $Q$ are plaques in distinct charts of $\mathfrak{A}$, then $P\cap Q$ is open both in $P$ and $Q$. 
	\end{definition}
	\begin{definition}\label{foli_coh_atlas_defn}
		Two foliated atlases lt and $\mathfrak{A}$ on $\mathfrak{A}'$ of the same codimension and smoothness class $C^r$ are \textit{coherent} ($\mathfrak{A}\approx\mathfrak{A}'$) if $\mathfrak{A}\cup\mathfrak{A}'$ is a foliated $C^*$-atlas.
	\end{definition}
	\begin{lemma}\label{foli_coh_atlas_eq_lem}
		Coherence of foliated atlases is an equivalence relation.
	\end{lemma}
	\begin{lemma}\label{foli_coh_atlas_ass_lem}
		Let  $\mathfrak{A}$ and $\mathfrak{A}'$ be foliated atlases on $M$ and suppose that $\mathfrak{A}$ is associated to a foliation $\sF$. Then $\mathfrak{A}$ and $\mathfrak{A}'$ are coherent if and only if $\mathfrak{A}'$ is also associated to $\sF$.
	\end{lemma}
	\begin{definition}\label{foli_reg_atlas_defn}
		A foliated atlas $\mathfrak{A}\bydef\left\{\sU_\a \right\}_{\a \in \mathscr A}$ of class $C^r$ is said to be \textit{regular} if
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item [(a)] For each $\al \in \mathscr A$, the closure $\overline{\sU}_\al$ of $\sU_\al$ is a compact subset of a foliated chart  $\left\{\sV_\a \right\}$ and $\varphi_\a = \psi|_{\sU_\a }$.
			\item[(b)] The cover $\left\{\sU_\a \right\}$ is locally finite.
			\item[(c)] if $\sU_\a$ and $\sU_\bt$ are elements of $\mathfrak{A}$, then the interior of each closed plaque $P \in \overline \sU_\a$ meets at most one plaque in $\overline \sU_\bt$.
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{definition}
	\begin{lemma}\label{foli_reg_atlas_ref_lem}
		Every foliated atlas has a coherent refinement that is regular.
	\end{lemma}
	\begin{thm}\label{foli_thm}
		The correspondence between foliations on $M$ and their associated foliated atlases induces a one-to-one correspondence between the set of foliations on $M$.
	\end{thm}
	
	We now have an alternative definition of the term "foliation". 
	\begin{defn}\label{foli_alt_defn}
		A \textit{foliation} $\sF$ of codimension $q$ and class $C^r$ on $M$ is a coherence class of foliated atlases of codimension $q$ and class $C^r$ on $M$.
	\end{defn}
	By Zorn's lemma \ref{zorn_thm}, it is obvious that every coherence class of foliated atlases contains a unique maximal foliated atlas. 
	\begin{defn}\label{foli_max_defn}
		A \textit{foliation of codimension} $q$ and class $C^r$ on $M$ is a maximal foliated $C^r$-atlas of codimension $q$ on $M$.
	\end{defn}
	
	\begin{empt}\label{foli_graph_empt}
		Let  $\Pi\left( M,\sF\right)$ be the space of paths on leaves, that is, maps $\a : [0,1] \to M$ that are continuous with respect to the leaf topology on $M$. For such a path  let $s\left(\a \right) = \a\left( 0\right)$  be its source or initial point and let  $r\left(\a \right) = \a\left( 1\right)$ be its range or terminal point. The space $\Pi\left( M,\sF\right)$ has a partially defined multiplication: the product $\a\cdot \bt$ of two elements $\a$ and $\bt$ is defined if the terminal point of $\bt$ is the initial point of $\a$, and the result is the path $\bt$ followed by the path $\a$. (Note that this is the opposite to the usual composition of paths  $\al\#\bt = \bt \cdot \a$ used in defining the fundamental group of a space.)
	\end{empt}
	\begin{definition}\label{foli_path_space_defn}
		In the situation of \ref{foli_graph_empt} we say that the topological space $\Pi\left( M,\sF\right)$ is the \textit{space of path on leaves}.
	\end{definition}
	\begin{definition}\label{foli_groupoid_defn}\cite{candel:foliI}
		%Definition 2.3.3.
		A groupoid $\G$ on a set $\sX$ is a category with inverses, having $\sX$ as its set of objects. For $y,z \in \sX$ the set of morphisms of $\G$ from $y$ to $z$ is denoted by $\G^z_y$.
	\end{definition}
	\begin{defn}\label{foli_graph_defn}\cite{candel:foliII}
		%	Definition 1.3.1
		The \textit{graph}, or \textit{my groupoid}, of the foliated space $\left( M,\sF\right)$  is the quotient space of $\Pi\left( M,\sF\right)$ by the equivalence relation that identifies two paths $\a$ and $\bt$ if they have the same initial and terminal points, and the loop $\a \cdot \bt$ has trivial germinal holonomy.
		The graph of $\left( M,\sF\right)$ will be denoted by $\G\left(M, \sF\right)$, or simply by  $\G\left( M\right)$ or by $\G$ when all other variables are understood.
	\end{defn}
	\begin{theorem}\label{foli_graph_thm}
		The graph $\G$ of $\left( M,\sF\right)$ is a groupoid with unit space $\G_0 = M$, and this algebraic structure is compatible with a foliated structure on $\G$ and $M$. Furthermore, the following properties hold.
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item [(i)] The range and source maps $r, s : \G \to M$ are topological submersions. 
			\item[(ii)] The inclusion of the unit space $M \to\G$ is a smooth map. 
			\item[(iii)] The product map $\G\times_M \G \to G$, given by $\left( \ga_1 , \ga_2\right) \mapsto\ga_1 \cdot \ga_2$, is smooth.
			\item[(iv)]  There is an involution $j: \G \to \G$, given by $j\left( \ga\right) = \ga^{-1}$, which is a diffeomorphism of $\G$, sends each leaf to itself, and exchanges the foliations given by the range. 
		\end{enumerate}
		
	\end{theorem}
	
	Above definitions refines the equivalence relation coming from
	the partition of $M$ in leaves $M = \cup L_{\alpha}$. 
	An element $\gamma$ of $\mathcal G$ is given by two points $x = s(\gamma)$,
	$y = r(\gamma)$ of $M$ together with an equivalence class of smooth
	paths: $\gamma (t)\in M$, $t \in [0,1]$; $\gamma (0) = x$, $\gamma
	(1) = y$, tangent to the bundle $\mathcal{F}$ ( i.e. with $\dot\gamma (t)
	\in \mathcal{F}_{\gamma (t)}$, $\forall \, t \in {\mathbb R}$) up to the
	following equivalence: $\gamma_1$ and $\gamma_2$ are equivalent if and only if
	the {\it my} of the path $\gamma_2 \circ \gamma_1^{-1}$ at the
	point $x$ is the {\it identity}. The graph $\mathcal G$ has an obvious
	composition law. For $\gamma , \gamma' \in G$, the composition
	$\gamma \circ \gamma'$ makes sense if $s(\gamma) = r(\gamma')$. If
	the leaf $L$ which contains both $x$ and $y$ has no my, then
	the class in $\mathcal G$ of the path $\gamma (t)$ only depends on the pair
	$(y,x)$. In general, if one fixes $x = s(\gamma)$, the map from $\mathcal G_x
	= \{ \gamma , s(\gamma) = x \}$ to the leaf $L$ through $x$, given
	by $\gamma \in \mathcal G_x \mapsto y = r(\gamma)$, is the my covering
	of $L$.
	Both maps $r$ and $s$ from the manifold $\mathcal G$ to $M$ are smooth
	submersions and the map $(r,s)$ to $M \times M$ is an immersion
	whose image in $M \times M$ is the (often singular) subset
	\begin{equation*}\label{subset}
		\{ (y,x)\in M \times M: \, \text{ $y$ and $x$ are on the same leaf}\}.
	\end{equation*}
	% We assume, for notational convenience, that the manifold $\mathcal G$ is Hausdorff, but as this fails to be the case in very interesting examples I shall refer to \cite{connes:foli_survey} for the removal of this hypothesis.  
	For
	$x\in M$ one lets $\Omega_x^{1/2}$ be the one dimensional complex
	vector space of maps from the exterior power $\wedge^k \,  \mathcal{F}_x$, $k =
	\dim F$, to ${\mathbb C}$ such that
	$$
	\rho \, (\lambda \, v) = \vert \lambda \vert^{1/2} \, \rho \, (v)
	\qquad \forall \, v \in \wedge^k \,  \mathcal{F}_x \, , \quad \forall \,
	\lambda \in {\mathbb R} \, .
	$$
	Then, for $\gamma \in\mathcal G$, one can identify $\Omega_{\gamma}^{1/2}$ with the one
	dimensional complex vector space $\Omega_y^{1/2} \otimes
	\Omega_x^{1/2}$, where $\gamma : x \to y$. In other words
	\be\label{foli_om_g_eqn}
	\Omega_{\mathcal G}^{1/2}=\, r^*(\Omega_M^{1/2})\otimes s^*(\Omega_M^{1/2})\,.
	\ee
	
	
	
	\begin{empt}\label{foli_sc_haus_empt}\cite{candel:foliII}
		The  groupoid of a foliated space all leaves of which are simply connected is Hausdorff.
	\end{empt}
	

	
	
	%	\begin{definition}\label{foli_graph_defn1}
		%		The  manifold $\mathcal G$ called the \textit{graph} (or \textit{my groupoid})
		%		of the foliation  $\left(M, \mathcal{F}\right)$  Denote by $\mathcal G\left(M, \mathcal{F}\right)$ the graph of  $\left(M, \mathcal{F}\right)$.
		%	\end{definition}
	
	\subsection{Operator algebras of foliations}\label{foli_alg_subsec}
	\paragraph*{}
	Here I follow to \cite{candel:foliII}.  Since the bundle $\Om^{1/2}$ is trivial (because $\G\left(M, \sF\right)$ admits partitions of unity), a choice of an everywhere positive density $\nu$ allows us to identify \\$\Ga^\infty_c\left(\G\left(M, \sF\right),\Om^{1/2}  \right)$  with $\Coo_c\left( \G\left(M, \sF\right)\right)$. 
	The definition of foliated space makes sense even when the underlying topological space fails to satisfy the Hausdorff separation axiom. Non-Hausdorff spaces appear naturally in the theory of foliations. In a graph of a foliated space is not necessary Hausdorff. For such a sheaf  $\A$ over $\sX$, let $\A$ denote its  resolution: $A'\left(\sU\right)$ is the set of all sections (continuous or not) of $\A$ over $\sU\subset\sX$. For a Hausdorff open subset $\mathcal W$ of $\sX$, let $\Ga^\infty_c\left( \mathcal W, \A\right) $ denote the set of continuous compactly supported sections of $\A$ over $\mathcal W$. If $\mathcal W\subset\sU$, then there is a well defined homomorphism $\Ga^\infty_c\left( \mathcal W, \A\right)\to \A'\left(\sU \right)$. For an open subset $\sU$ of $\sX$, let $\Ga^\infty_c\left( \mathcal U, \A\right)$ denote the image of the homomorphism $\oplus \Ga^\infty_c\left( \mathcal W, \A\right)\to \A'\left(\sU\right)$  it follows that there is the inclusion
	\be\label{foli_incc_eqn}
	\Ga^\infty_c\left(\mathcal W, \A \right) \hookto \Ga^\infty_c\left(\mathcal U, \A \right).
	\ee
	Let $\G\bydef \G\left(M, \sF \right)$ be a foliation chart. 	The bundle $\Omega_M^{1/2}$ is trivial on $M$, and we
	could choose once and for all  a trivialisation $\nu$ turning
	elements of $\Ga^\infty_c \left(\mathcal G , \Omega_{\mathcal G}^{1/2}\right)$ into functions.
	Let us
	however stress that the usage of half densities makes all the
	construction completely canonical.
	For $f,g \in \Ga^\infty_c \left(\mathcal G , \Omega_{\mathcal G}^{1/2}\right)$, the convolution
	product $f * g$ is defined by the equality
	\be\label{foli_prod_eqn}
	(f * g) (\gamma) = \int_{\gamma_1 \circ \gamma_2 = \gamma}
	f(\gamma_1) \, g(\gamma_2) \, .
	\ee
	This makes sense because, for fixed $\gamma : x \to y$ and fixing $v_x
	\in \wedge^k \,  \mathcal{F}_x$ and $v_y \in \wedge^k \,  \mathcal{F}_y$, the product
	$f(\gamma_1) \, g(\gamma_1^{-1} \gamma)$ defines a $1$-density on
	$G^y = \{ \gamma_1 \in G , \, r (\gamma_1) = y \}$, which is smooth
	with compact support (it vanishes if $\gamma_1 \notin\supp f$),
	and hence can be integrated over $G^y$ to give a scalar, namely $(f * g)
	(\gamma)$ evaluated on $v_x , v_y$.
	The $*$ operation is defined by $f^* (\gamma) =
	\overline{f(\gamma^{-1})}$,  i.e. if $\gamma : x \to y$ and
	$v_x \in \wedge^k \, \mathcal{F}_x$, $v_y \in \wedge^k \, \mathcal{F}_y$ then $f^*
	(\gamma)$ evaluated on $v_x , v_y$ is equal to
	$\overline{f(\gamma^{-1})}$ evaluated on $v_y , v_x$. We thus get a
	$*$-algebra $\Ga^\infty_c \left(\mathcal G , \Omega_{\mathcal G}^{1/2}\right)$. 
	where $\xi$ is a square integrable half density on $\mathcal G_x$. 
	For each leaf $L$ of
	$\left(M, \mathcal{F}\right)$ one has a natural representation of this $*$-algebra on the
	$L^2$ space of the my covering $\tilde L$ of $L$. Fixing a
	base point $x \in L$, one identifies $\tilde L$ with $\mathcal G_x = \{
	\gamma , s(\gamma) = x \}$ and defines
	\begin{equation}\label{foli_repr_eqn}
		(\rho_x (f) \, \xi) \, (\gamma) = \int_{\gamma_1 \circ \gamma_2 =
			\gamma} f(\gamma_1) \, \xi (\gamma_2) \qquad \forall \, \xi \in L^2
		(\mathcal G_x),\
	\end{equation}
	
	
	Given
	$\gamma : x \to y$ one has a natural isometry of $L^2 (\mathcal G_x)$ on $L^2
	(G_y)$ which transforms the representation $\rho_x$ in $\rho_y$.
	\begin{lemma}
		%	Lemma 1.4.1. \\
		If $f_1 \in \Ga^\infty_c\left(\sU_{   \a_1},\Om^{1/2} \right)$ and $f_2 \in \Ga^\infty_c\left(\sU_{   \a_2},\Om^{1/2} \right)$ then their convolution is a well-defined element $f_1*f_2 \in \Ga^\infty_c\left(\sU_{   \a_1}\cdot\sU_{   \a_2},\Om^{1/2} \right)$
	\end{lemma}
	%page 24.
	%Let $\Ga^\infty_c\left( \G,\Om^{1/2}\right)$  be the space of compactly supported smooth sections of $\Om^{1/2}$ over $\G$; its elements are the compactly supported half-densities on G. When G is not Hausdorff, the meaning of c (G,D1/2) is that which was described in Section 1.2. This space will now be given the structure of an algebra with involution. This structure is first described when G is Hausdorff, and the details will then be given when G is arbitrary.
	
	\begin{proposition}\label{foli_repr_prop}
		%Proposition 1.4.5. \\
		If $\sV \subset \G$ is a foliated chart for the graph of $\left(M, \sF\right)$ and $f \in \Ga^\infty_c\left(\sV, \Om^{1/2}\right)$ , then $\rho_x\left( f\right)$, given by \eqref{foli_repr_eqn}, is a bounded integral operator on $L^2\left(\G_x \right)$.
	\end{proposition}
	\begin{empt}
		The space of compactly supported half-densities on $\G$ is taken as given by the exact sequence 
		\be\label{foli_ga_p_eqn}
		\bigoplus_{\a_0\a_1}\Ga^\infty_c\left(\sU_{   \a_0\a_1}, \Om^{1/2} \right) \to \bigoplus_{\a_0}\Ga^\infty_c\left(\sU_{   \a_0},\Om^{1/2} \right) \xrightarrow{\Ga_\oplus}  \Ga^\infty_c\left(\G,\Om^{1/2}\right) 
		\ee
		associated to a regular cover for $\left((M, \sF)\right)$ as above. The first step for defining a convolution is to do it at the level of $\bigoplus_{\a_0}\Ga^\infty_c\left(\sU_{   \a_0}\Om^{1/2} \right)$, as the following lemma indicates. 
	\end{empt}
	
	
	
	
	\begin{defn}\label{foli_red_defn}\cite{candel:foliII}
		% 	Definition 1.4.7.\\
		The \textit{reduced} $C^*$-algebra of the foliated space $\left(M,\sF\right)$ is the completion of $\Ga^\infty_c\left( \G,\Om^{1/2}\right)$ with respect to the pseudonorm \be\label{foli_pseudo_norm_eqn}
		\left\|f \right\| =\sup_{x \in M}\left\|  \rho_x\left(f\right)\right\|
		\ee where $\rho_x$ is given by  \eqref{foli_repr_eqn}.
		This $C^*$-algebra is denoted by $C^*_r\left(M,\sF\right)$.
	\end{defn}
	\begin{empt}\label{foli_res_inc_empt}\cite{candel:foliII}
		%	page 29-30.
		Let $\left(M,\sF\right)$ be an arbitrary foliated space and let $\sU\subset  M$ be an open subset.  Then $\left(\sU,\sF|_\sU\right)$ is a foliated space and the inclusion  $\sU\hookto  M$ induces a homomorphism of groupoids $\G\left(\sU \right)\hookto \G$ , hence a mapping
		\be\label{foli_inc_gc_eqn}
		j_\sU : \Ga^\infty_c\left(  \G\left(\sU \right) ,\Om^{1/2}\right)  \hookto \Ga^\infty_c\left( \G\left( M\right) ,\Om^{1/2}\right)
		\ee
		that is an injective homomorphism of involutive algebras. 
		%	It is proven in \cite{candel:foliII,connes:foli_survey} that any restriction of foliation induces an injective *-homomorphism 
		%	\begin{equation}\label{fol_res_hom_eqn}
			%	C^*_r\left(\mathcal{U},\mathcal F|_{\mathcal{U}} \right)\hookto C^*_r\left(M,\mathcal F \right).
			%	\end{equation}
	\end{empt}
	
	An obvious consequence of the construction of 
	$C^*_r\left(M,\sF\right)$ is the following. 
	
	\begin{cor}\label{foli_cov_alg_cor}\cite{candel:foliII}
		%	Corollary 1.4.8.
		Let $M$ be a foliated space and let $\mathfrak{A}$ be a regular cover by foliated charts. Then the algebra generated by the convolution algebras $\Ga^\infty_c\left( \G\left(\sU \right), \Om^{1/2}\right)$, $~\sU\in\mathfrak{A}$ is dense in  $C^*_r\left(M,\sF\right)$.
	\end{cor}
	\begin{prop}\label{foli_res_inc_prop}\cite{candel:foliII} %	Proposition 1.5.5. 
		Let $\sU$ be an open subset of the foliated space $M$. Then the inclusion $\sU \hookto M$ induces an isometry of $C^*_r\left(\sU,\sF|_\sU\right)$  into $C^*_r\left(M,\sF\right)$.
	\end{prop}
	
	\begin{lem}\label{foli_point_lem}\cite{candel:foliII}
		%Lemma 1.4.10. 
		If $f \in \Ga^\infty_c\left(\G, \Om^{1/2}\right)$ does not evaluate to zero at each $\ga \in \G$, then there exists a point $x$ in $M$ such that $\rho_x\left( f\right)  \neq  0$.
	\end{lem}
	
	\begin{definition}\label{foli_fibration_defn}\cite{candel:foliI}
		A foliated space $\left(M, \sF\right)$ is a \textit{fibration} if for any $x$ there is an open transversal $N$ such that $x\in N$ and for every  leaf $L$ of $\left(M, \sF\right)$ the intersection $L\cap  N$ contains no more then one point.
	\end{definition}
	
	
	
	\begin{prop}\label{foli_tens_comp_prop}\cite{candel:foliII}
		% Proposition 1.5.4. \\
		The reduced  $C^*$-algebra $C^*_r\left(\mathcal N\times \mathcal Z \right)$ of the trivial foliated space $\mathcal N \times \mathcal Z$ is the tensor product $\K\otimes C_0\left(\mathcal Z\right)$, where $\K$ is the algebra of compact operators on $L^2\left(\mathcal N \right)$  and $ C_0\left(\mathcal Z\right)$ is the space of continuous functions on $\mathcal Z$ that vanish at infinity.
	\end{prop}
	
	
	%\begin{definition}\label{foli_pseudo_a_defn}\cite{candel:foliII}
	%Definition 1.3.6.\\ 
	%The my pseudogroup of a foliated space is \textit{pseudo analytic} if the following holds. If $h: Z\to Z'$ is a my transformation between two transversals $Z$ and $Z'$, with $Z\subset Z'$, and $W \subset Z$ is an open subset such that $\left.h\right|_W = \Id$, and if $x \in \overline{W}$ is such that $h(x) = x$, then $h = \Id$ on a neighborhood of x. 
	%\end{definition}
	
	%\begin{proposition}\label{foli_pseudo_a_prop}\cite{candel:foliII}
	%Proposition 1.3.7. \\
	%Let M be a foliated space. Then the graph of M is Hausdorff if and only if the my pseudogroup of M is pseudo-analytic.
	%\end{proposition}
%	\begin{theorem}\label{foli_irred_hol_thm} \cite{candel:foliII}
		%Theorem 1.4.11. 
%		Let $(M,\sF)$ be a foliated space and let $x \in M$. Then the representation $\rho_x$ is irreducible if and only if the leaf through $x$ has no holonomy.
%	\end{theorem}
	
	
	\begin{empt}\label{foli_irred_hol_empt}\cite{candel:foliII}
	Let $(M,\sF)$ be a foliated space. Any irreducible representation of $C^*_r(M,\sF)$ corresponds to a leaf $\L$ of  $(M,\sF)$ such that $\L$ has no holonomy. The Hilbert space of the representation is $L^2\left( \L\right)$. 
	\end{empt}
	
%			\begin{empt}\label{foli_haar_empt}
%		If $\a$ be a continuous 2-cocycle in $Z^2\left(\G\left(M, \F \right) , \T\right)$ then similarly to \ref{groupoid_haar_empt} one can define a *-algebra
%		$$
%		C_c\left(M, \F, \a \right) \bydef C_c\left(\G\left(M, \F \right) , \a \right) 
%		$$
%		with the given by the equations \eqref{groupoid_*_c_eqn} and \eqref{groupoid_inv_c_eqn} operations. Let $C^*_r\left(M, \F, \a \right)$ be the completion of $C_c\left(M, \F, \a \right)$ with respect to the norm \eqref{groupoid_norm_eqn}. From the Statement \ref{gropoid_mult_lem}  there is the natural inclusion 		$C_0\left(M \right) \hookto M\left( C^*_r\left(M, \F, \a\right)\right)$.
%	\end{empt}
	
\section{Bohr locales}
\paragraph*{}
The aim of Bohr toposes is to relate algebraic quantum mechanics to
topos theory, so as to construct new foundations for quantum logic
and quantum spaces.	Bohr toposes provide the topos-theoretical notion of a space which
intrinsically carries the intuitionistic logical structure of a Heyting algebra. We need the following.
\begin{definition}\label{alex_top_defn}\cite{johnstone:stone_spaces}.
	Let $X$ be a partially ordered set. We define the \textit{Alexandrov topology} $\Upsilon(X, \le)$ (or simply 
	$\Upsilon(X)$, if the partial order is obvious) to be the collection of all \textit{upper sets}
	in $X$ (i.e. sets $U$ such that $x \in  U$ and $x \le y$ imply $y \in U$); this is clearly a 
	topology, since it is closed under arbitrary unions and intersections. 
	
\end{definition}

\begin{remark}\label{alex_not_sober}\cite{beyond_sober_duality}
	Alexandrov topology are not always sober.
\end{remark} 
For any $C^*$-algebra 
one can consider the partially ordered set $\mathcal C(A)$  of commutative
subalgebras of $A$, ordered by inclusion. The assignment of this partially ordered to
a $C^*$-algebra induces a functor $\mathcal C$ from the category of $C^*$-algebras to the category of partially ordered sets, since one has for any
homomorphism $A \xrightarrow{h}B$ a functor $
\mathcal C(A)\xrightarrow{\mathcal C}  \mathcal C(B)$
\newline
\begin{tikzcd}
	C \arrow[d] \arrow[r, mapsto]
	& h(C) \arrow[d]\\
	B\arrow[r, mapsto]	&h (B)
\end{tikzcd}
\\
\\
A partially ordered set yields an Alexandrov space $L(A)$.
\begin{definition}\label{bohr_topos_defn}\cite{joost:borification}
	The \textit{Bohr topos} of $C^*$-algebra $A$ is a  topos sheaves $\mathbf{Sh}\left( L(A)\right)$ over an Alexandrov space $L(A)$.
\end{definition}
\begin{remark}
	The open sets of the Alexandrov space is a partially ordered set, so from the Theorem \ref{locale_thm} it follows that the {Bohr topos} is localic. 
\end{remark}
\begin{definition}\label{bohr_locale_defn}
	The locale of the {Bohr topos} of $C^*$-algebra $A$ is said to be the \textit{Bohr} $A$-\textit{locale}, denoted by $\mathfrak{BohrLocale}(A)$.
\end{definition}
\begin{remark}\label{bohr_not_spat_rem}
	From the Remark \ref{alex_not_sober} and the Proposition \ref{sober_prop} it follows that the {Bohr topos} should not be always spatial.
\end{remark}
	
\section{Algebraic geometry}
\paragraph{}
In this section we will consider schemes satisfying the following condition: 
\\
(*) $\sX$ is a Noetherian integral separated scheme which is regular in codimension one. Details of this notion is explained in \cite{hartshorne:ag}. 
\begin{remark}\label{ag_weil_complex_rem}
	For any natural $n$ a complex projective space $\C P^n$ satisfies to the condition (*).
\end{remark}


\subsection{Weil divisors}

\begin{definition}\label{ag_weil_div_defn}\cite{hartshorne:ag}
Let X satisfy (*). A \textit{prime divisor} on $D$ is a closed integral sub-
scheme $\sY$ of codimension one. A \textit{Weil divisor} is an element of the free Abelian group $\mathrm{Div}~ \sX$ generated by the prime divisors. We write a divisor 
as $D = \sum n_j \sY_j$  where the $\sY_j$ are prime divisors, the $n_j$ are integers, and only finitely many $n_j$ are different from zero. If all the $n_j \ge  0$, we say 
that $D$ is \textit{effective}. 
\end{definition}
\begin{empt}\cite{hartshorne:ag}
The used below notions are explained in \cite{hartshorne:ag}. If $\sY$ is a prime divisor of $\sX$, let $\eta \in \sY$ be its generic point. Then the local ring $\mathscr O_{\eta, \sX}$ is a discrete valuation ring with quotient field $K$, the function field of $\sX$. We call the corresponding valuation $v_\sY$ the \textit{valuation of} $\sY$. Since $\sX$ is separated, $\sY$ is uniquely  determined by its valuation. Now let $f \in K^*$ be any nonzero rational function on $\sX$. Then $v_\sY\left(f\right)$ is an integer. If it positive, we say that $f$ has a \text{zero} along $\sY$; if it is negative, we say that $f$ has a \textit{pole} along $\sY$ of order $-v_\sY\left(f \right)$.  
\end{empt}
\begin{lemma}\label{ag_weil_div_lem}\cite{hartshorne:ag}
Let $\sX$ satisfy (*), and let $f\in K^*$ be a nonzero function on $\sX$. 
Then $v_\sY\left(f \right)= 0$  for all except finitely many prime divisors $\sY$. 
\end{lemma}
\begin{definition}
Let $\sX$ satisfy (*) and let fe $K^*$. We define the \textit{divisor} of $f$, denoted $(f)$, by 
$$
(f)\bydef \sum v_\sY\left( f\right) \cdot \sY,
$$
where the sum is taken over all prime divisors of $\sX$. By the lemma, this 
is a finite sum, hence it is a divisor. Any divisor which is equal to the 
divisor of a function is called a \textit{principal} divisor. 
\end{definition}
Note that if $f,g \in  K^*$, then $(f/g) = (f)  (g$) because of the properties of valuations. Therefore sending a function $f$ to its divisor $(f)$ gives a homomorphism of the multiplicative group  $K^*$ to the additive group $\mathrm{Div}~ \sX$, and the image, which consists of the principal divisors, is a subgroup of $\mathrm{Div}~ \sX$. 
\begin{definition}
Let $\sX$ satisfy (*). Two divisors $D$ and $D'$ are said to be \textit{linearly}\label{ag_div_class_gr_defn}\cite{hartshorne:ag}
equivalent, written $D \approx D$ if $D - D'$ is a principal divisor. The group $\mathrm{Div} ~\sX$ of all divisors divided by the subgroup of principal divisors is 
called the \textit{divisor class group} of $\sX$, and is denoted by $\mathrm{Cl}~ \sX$. 
\end{definition}
\subsection{Intersection theory}
\paragraph{}
Let $\sX$ be any variety over a field  $k$. A \textit{cycle of codimension} $r$ on $\sX$ is an element of the free Abelian group generated by the closed irreducible subvarieties of $\sX$ 
of codimension $r$. So we write a cycle as $Y = \sum n_j \sY_j$ where the $\sY_j$ are sub	varieties, and $n_j \in \Z$. Sometimes it is useful to speak of the cycle associated 
	to a closed subscheme. If $Z$ is a closed subscheme of codimension $r$ let  $\sY_1, ..., \sY_l$ be those irreducible components of $\sZ$ which have codimension $r$, 	and define the cycle associated to $\sZ$ to be $\sum n_j \sY_j$, where $n_j$ is the length of 
		the local ring $\mathscr O_{y_j, \sZ}$ of the generic point $y_j$ of $\sY_j$ on $\sZ$. 
			Let $f: \sX \to \sX'$ be a morphism of varieties, and let $\sY$ be a subvariety of $\sX$. 	If $\dim f(\sY) < \dim \sY$, we set $f_*\left( \sY\right) = 0$. If $\dim f(\sY) = \dim \sY$, then the 			function field $K(\sY)$ is a finite extension field of $K(f(\sY))$, and we set 
			$$
			f_*(\sY )\bydef \left[K(\sY) : K\left(f(\sY) \right)  \right]\cdot \overline{f (\sY)} .
			$$
						 
	Now we come to the definition of rational equivalence For any subvariety 	$\sV$ of $\sX$, let $f: \tilde \sV \to \sV$ be the normalization of $\sV$. Then $\sV$ satisfies the condition (*), so we can talk about Weil divisors and linear equivalence 
	on $\widetilde \sV$. Whenever $D$ and $B'$ are linearly equivalent Weil divisors on $\widetilde \sV$, we say 
	that $f_*D$ and $f_*D'$  are \textit{rationally equivalent} as cycles on $\sX$. Then we define 
	rational equivalence of cycles on $\sX$ in general by dividing out by the group 
	generated by all such $f_*D \cong f_*D'$ for all subvarieties $\sV$, and all linearly 
	equivalent Weil divisors $D,D'$ on $\sV$. In particular, if X itself is normal, then 
	rational equivalence for cycles of codimension 1 coincides with linear 
	equivalence of Weil divisors. 
	For each $r$ we let $A^r(\sX)$ be the group of cycles of codimension $r$ on $\sX$  
	modulo rational equivalence. We denote by $A(\sX)$ the graded group $\bigoplus_{r=0}^n A^r(\sX)$,
where $n = \dim \sX$. Note that $A^0(\sX)= \Z$, and that $A^r(\sX) = \{0\}$ for 	$r > \dim X$. 
	%Note also that if X is complete there is a natural group homo	morphism, the degree, from An(X) to Z, defined by deg(^/7I-Pi) = ?/7f, where 	the Pt are points. This is well-defined on rational equivalence classes because 
\begin{remark}\label{ag_chow_ring_rem}
If $\sX$ is a 	nonsingular projective variety  then there is an explained in \cite{hartshorne:ag} pairing $A^r(\sX) \times A^s(\sX)\to A^{r + s}(\sX)$ for each $r,s$. If $\sY \in A^r(\sX)$ and $\sZ \in A^s(\sX)$ we denote the pairing  cycle class by $\sY.\sZ$. If $A(\sX)\bydef \bigoplus A^r(\sX)$ then this pairing makes $A(\sX)$ into a commutative associative 
graded ring with identity. It is called the \text{Chow ring} of $\sX$. Roughly speaking  $\sY.\sZ$ correspond   to the  intersection $\sY\cap\sZ$ of subvarieties. Below we denote by $\left[\sV\right]$ an element of the Chow ring  represented by subvariety $\sV \subset \sX$.
\end{remark}
\begin{empt}
For an $n$-dimensional non-singular variety $\sY$, set
\be
A^p \left(\sY  \right)\bydef A_{n-p}\left(\sY \right).  
\ee
With this indexing by codimension, we have
$$
A^p \left(\sY  \right)\otimes A^q \left(\sY  \right)\to A^{p+q}\left( \sY\right) 
$$
\end{empt}
 
%	Before stating the axioms, for any morphism f:X -> X' of varieties in 93, 	we assume that X x X' is also in 93, and we define a homomorphism 	f*:A(X') -> A(X) as follows. For a subvariety Y' c X' we define 	where px and p2 are the projections of X x X' to X and X', and T^ is the 	graph of/, considered as a cycle on X x X'. 

\subsection{Chow ring and homology}
\paragraph{} 
Here I follow to \cite{fulton:intersection}. Each $k$-dimensional complex variety $\sV$ has a cycle class ${cl}(\sV)$  in $H_{2k}\left( \sV\right)$, where 
$H_*$ denotes homology with locally finite supports (Borel-Moore homology). If $\sV$ is a subvariety of an $n$-dimensional complex manifold $\sX$, then $H_{2k}(\sV) \cong H^{2n - 2k} \left(\sX \setminus \sV \right)$ 
The resulting homomorphism from cycles to homology 
passes to algebraic equivalence. There results in particular a cycle map
\be
cl : A_*\left(\sX  \right) \to H_*\left( \sX\right) 
\ee
for complex schemes $\sX$, which is covariant for proper morphisms, and 
compatible with Chern classes of vector bundles. 
If $\sV$ and $\mathcal W$ are subvarieties of dimensions $k$ and $l$ of a non-singular $n$-dimensional variety $\sX$, a refined topological intersection product $cl\left(\sV \right)\cdot cl\left( \mathcal W\right)$   
is constructed in $H_{2m}(\sV \cap \mathcal W)$, $m = k + l-n$. If $cl^\sX (\sV)$is the class in 
$H^{2n - 2k}(\sX,\sX \setminus \sV)$ dual to $cl(\sV)$, and similarly for $cl^\sX(\mathcal W)$, then $cl(\sV)\dot(\mathcal W)$
		is defined to be the class dual to 
		$$
		cl^\sX\left( \sV\right) \smile 	cl^\sX\left( \mathcal W\right)\in H^{2n - 2m}\left(\sX, \sX \setminus \sV \cap \mathcal W \right). 
		$$
Borel-Moore homology can be defined using singular cohomology. If a 
space $\sX$ is imbedded as a closed subspace of $\R^n$, then 
\be\label{chow1_eqn}
H_j\left(\sX  \right) \cong H^{n-j}\left(\R^n, \R^n \setminus \sX \right) 
\ee
where the group on the right is relative singular cohomology with integer 
coefficients. More generally, if $\sX$ is a closed subspace of a space $\sY$, there are 
cap products 
\be\label{chow2_eqn}
H^j\left(\sY, \sY \setminus\sX \right) \otimes \xrightarrow{\frown}H_{k-j}\left(\sX \right). 
\ee
If $\sX$ is an oriented, connected, real $n$-manifold, then $H_n\left( \sY\right)$ is freely generated by 
a \textit{fundamental class} $\mu_\sY$, and capping with $\mu_\sY$ determines an isomorphism 
\be\label{chow3_eqn}
H^{n-j}\left(\sY, \sY \setminus \sX \right) \xrightarrow[\cong]{\frown \mu_\sY}H_{k-j}\left( \sX\right). 
\ee
When $\sY = \R^n$, this is the isomorphism of \eqref{chow1_eqn}. When $\sX = \sY$, \eqref{chow3_eqn} is the Poincar\'{e} duality isomorphism of $H^{n-j}\left(\sY \right)$  with $H_{j}\left(\sY \right)$. 
If $f : \sY \to \sX$ is a proper morphism, there are covariant homomorphisms 
\be\label{chow4_eqn}
f_* : H_{j}\left(\sX \right) \to H_{j}\left(\sY \right) 
\ee
If $j: \sU \to \sY$ is an open embedding, there are contravariant restriction homomorphisms
\be\label{chow5_eqn}
j^* : H_{p}\left(\sY \right) \to H_{p}\left(\sU \right). 
\ee
 If $\sX$ is the complement of $\sU$ in $\sY$, $i: \sX\to \sY$ the closed embedding, there is a long exact sequence 
 \be\label{chow6_eqn}
 ...\to  H_{p+1}\left(\sU \right) \to H_{p}\left(\sX \right)\xrightarrow{i_*} \H_{p}\left(\sY \right)\xrightarrow{j_*}H_{p}\left(\sU \right)\to H_{p-1}\left(\sX \right)\to .... 
 \ee
  If $\sX$ is a disjoint union of a finite number of spaces $\sX_1,..., \sX_m$, then 
 \be\label{chow7_eqn}
H_{i}\left(\sX \right) = H_{i}\left(\sX_1 \right) \oplus ...\oplus H_{i}\left(\sX_m \right)
 \ee 
 There are evident compatibilities among these homomorphisms, which will be used without comment. One such is the equation  
 \be\label{chow8_eqn}
\left( u \smile v \right) \frown a = j^*\left( u\right)\frown \left( v \frown a\right)  
 \ee 
 in $H_*\left( \sX \cap \mathcal W\right)$ , where $\sX \hookto \sY$, $~j:\mathcal W \to  \sY$ are closed embeddings, $u \in H^*(\sY, \sY\setminus \sX), ~v \in H^*(\sY, \sY- \mathcal W), ~a \in H_*\left( \sY\right)$. 
 \begin{lemma}\cite{fulton:intersection}
% Lemma 19.1.1. 
 If $\sX$ is an $n$-dimensional complex scheme, then $H^j(\sX) =\{0\}$ for 
 $i >2n$, and $H_{2n}\left(\sX \right) $ is a free Abelian group with one generator for each irreducible 
 component of $\sX$. 
 \end{lemma}
 \begin{definition}
 The generator of $H_{2n}\left(\sX \right)$ corresponding to an $n$-dimensional irreducible 
 component $\sX_i$, will be denoted $cl(\sX_i)$; it is determined by the fact that it 
 restricts to the fundamental class $\mu_{\sU_i}$ for any connected open subset $\sU_i$, of the 
 non-singular locus of $\sX_i$. More generally, if $\sV$ is a $k$-dimensional closed sub variety of a scheme $\sX$, define the \textit{cycle class} $cl_\sX(V)$ by 
 \be
 cl_\sX\left(\sV \right) \bydef i_* cl\left(\sV\right)\in H_{2k}\left( \sX\right) 
 \ee
 where $i$ is the inclusion of $\sV$ in $\sX$. When no confusion is likely, we may write $cl (\sV)$ 
 in place of $cl_\sX\left( V\right)$. It is proven in \cite{fulton:intersection}. For any $\sX$, there is a homomorphism denoted $cl$, or $cl_\sX$, 
 \bean
 A_*(\sX )\to H_*\left(\sX \right),\\
 \sum_i n_i \left[\sV_i\right] \mapsto \sum_i n_i cl\left(\sV_i\right).
 \eean 
 \end{definition}
 \begin{definition}\label{ad_ori_defn}
Every regular (closed) embedding $i: \sV\hookto\sX$ of codimension $d$ has an \textit{orientation class}.
\be\label{ad_ori_eqn}
u_i = u_{\sV, \sX}\in H^{2d}\left(\sX, \sX \setminus \sV \right) 
\ee 
 \end{definition}
There is a long exact sequence of cohomology
 \be
 \begin{split}
 	...\to  H^{p-1}\left(\sV ; \Z\right) \to H^{p}\left(\sX, \sX\setminus\sV ;\Z\right)\xrightarrow{i^*_p} H^{p}\left(\sX;\Z \right)\xrightarrow{j^*_p}H^{p}\left(\sV ; \Z\right)\to\\ \to H^{p+1}\left(\sX, \sX\setminus\sV ; \Z\right)\to .... 
 \end{split}
 \ee
 There is an Abelian group homomorphism $cl: A^*\left( \sX\right)  \to H^*\left(\sX \right)$ such that if $\sV$ is an irreducible $k$-dimensional submanifold of $\sX$ then
 \be
 cl \left(\left[\sY\right] \right) = i^*_{2d}\left(u_{\sV, \sX} \right)\in H^{2d} \left(\sX \right)
 \ee 

 
 \begin{corollary}
 	%19.2
 The mapping $cl: A^*\left( \sX\right)  \to H^*\left(\sX \right)$ is a homomorphism of graded rings, covariant for morphism of non-singular varieties. 
 \end{corollary}
\end{appendices}
\section{APP}
\section{Topoi of the $C^*$-algebras of compact operators}
\paragraph{}
Let $p \in \N\cup \infty$
In this section we use  following notations:
\begin{itemize}
	\item 
	$$
	\H \bydef \begin{cases}
		\C P^p & p \in \N\\
		\ell^2\left(\N \right) & p = \infty
	\end{cases}.
	$$	
	\item  $\pi:\K \bydef \K\left(\H \right)$ the $C^*$-algebra of compact operators. 
\end{itemize}
The natural representation $\pi:\K \to B\left(\H \right)$ is irreducible. 
\subsection{Sheaves and cohomology}
\begin{lemma}\label{compact_hered_lem}
	If $B \subset \K$ is a hereditary $C^*$-subalgebra then $B \cong \K\left(\H_B\right)$ where $\H_B \bydef \pi\left(B \right)  \H$. 
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
	The $C^*$-algebra $\K$ has the single irreducible representation $\pi : A \to B\left(\H\right)$. The *-homomorphism $\pi$ is injective so  $B\not\subset \ker \pi$, so from the Lemma \ref{hered_irred_lem} it follows that $\pi|_B : B \to B\left(\H_B\right)$  is an irreducible representation of $B$. From the condition (iv) of the Theorem  \ref{irred_thm} it follows that for any two vectors $\xi, \eta \in \H_B$ with $\xi \neq 0$ there is $b \in B$ such that $\pi\left(b \right)\xi = \eta$. From this fact and taking into account that $b \in B$ is compact one concludes that $B = \K\left( \H_B\right)$. 
\end{proof}
\begin{empt}
	From the Lemma \ref{compact_hered_lem} the map $B \mapsto \H_B$ yields one-to-one correspondence between hereditary $C^*$-subalgebras of $\K$ and subspaces of $\H$ such that
	\begin{enumerate}
		\item  $B' \cap B''$ corresponds to $\H_{B'}\cap \H_{B''}$,
		\item A generated by   $B' \cup B''$ hereditary $C^*$-subalgebra  of $\K$ corresponds to  $\H_{B'} + \H_{B''}$.
	\end{enumerate}
	Now we can construct a specialization of given by the section \ref{groth_top_sec} construction. A family  $\mathbf{Hered}/\K$-objects is naturally isomorphic to the family of closed subspaces of $\H$.  A $\mathbf{Hered}/\K$ morphism $\H' \to \H''$ corresponds to an inclusion $\H'' \subset \H'$. The fiber product $\H' \times_{\{0\}} \H''$ corresponds to $\H' + \H''$. A covering of $\H'$ is a finite set $\left\{\H_1, ..., \H_n\right\}$ of subspaces  such that $\H'= \bigcap_{j = 1}^n\H_j$. So one has a pretopology denoted by $J_\K$, and the Gelfand $\K$-site $\mathfrak{Site}\left(\K \right) = \left(\mathbf{Hered}/\K, J_\K \right)$. 
\end{empt}
\begin{empt}
	There is an alternative description of $\mathfrak{Site}\left(\K \right)$. Instead to Hilbert spaces we set their orthogonal complements.   A $\mathbf{Hered}/\K$ morphism $\H' \to \H''$ corresponds to an inclusion $\H' \subset \H''$. The fiber product $\H' \times_{\{0\}} \H''$ corresponds to $\H' \cap  \H''$. A covering of $\H'$ is a finite set $\left\{\H_1, ..., \H_n\right\}$ of subspaces  such that $\H'= \sum_{j = 1}^n\H_j$. So one has  an alternative  pretopology denoted by $J_\K$, and the Gelfand $\K$-site $\mathfrak{Site}\left(\K \right) = \left(\mathbf{Hered}/\K, J_\K \right)$. Henceforth this alternative topology will be used.
\end{empt}
\begin{empt}
	Let $R$ be a ring ans let $R_\K$ be a constant presheaf (cf. Definition \ref{constant_presheaf_defn}). If $\mathscr R \bydef \mathfrak {Ass}\left( R_\K\right)$ is the associated sheaf (cf. Definition \ref{associated_sheaf_defn}). If $\H' \subset \H$ is a $\C$-linear subspace  then $\H'$ can be regarded as a $\mathbf{Hered}/\K$-object and  according to 
	to  the Statement \ref{associated_sheaf_defn} any $s \in \mathscr R$ can be represented by a finite family $\left\{\left(\H_1, r_1 \right),..., \left(\H_k, r_k \right) \right\}\subset  \mathbf{Hered}/\K \times R$ such that:
	\bean
	\sum_{j = 1}^k \H_j = \H',\\
	\H_{j_1}\cap \H_{j_2} \neq \{0\}\quad \Rightarrow \quad r_{j_1}= r_{j_2}.
	\eean
	It turns out that any  $s \in \mathscr R$ can be represented by a finite family $\left\{\left(\H_1, r_1 \right),..., \left(\H_k, r_k \right) \right\}\subset  \mathbf{Hered}/\K \times R$ such that:
	\be\label{compact_sh_eqn}
	\begin{split}
		\sum_{j = 1}^k \H_j = \H',\\
		j_1 \neq j_2 \quad \Rightarrow \quad \H_{j_1}\cap \H_{j_2} = \{0\}
	\end{split}
	\ee
\end{empt}
\begin{remark}
	From the equation \ref{compact_sh_eqn} it follows that  $\mathscr R$ can be regarded as flabby sheaf, i.e.
	\bean
	\forall \H' \in \H \quad \forall s' \in \mathscr R\left( \H'\right)\quad   \exists  s \in \mathscr R\left( \H\right)\quad 
	\eean 
\end{remark}

\

\begin{lemma}\label{sh_coo_lem} !!!! MAY BE DELETE !!!
	Let $\sX$ be a compact locally connected space, and let $R$ is a principal ideal domain. Suppose there is an injective continuous map $\sX \hookto\sY$. If $R_\sY$ is a constant presheaf (cf. Definition \ref{constant_presheaf_defn}) and $\mathscr L \bydef \mathfrak{Ass}\left( R_\sY\right)$ ia an associated sheaf  then following conditions hold:
	\begin{enumerate}
		\item [(i)] 	For any open $\sU \in \sX$ there are natural isomorphisms
		\be\label{sh_co_eqn}
		\varphi_\sU:	\mathscr L\left(\sU \right) \cong \mathfrak D \left( \mathscr L \right) \left(\sU \right)  
		\ee
		where $\mathfrak D \left( \mathscr L \right)$ is a dual cosheaf
		$$
		\mathfrak D \left( \mathscr L \right)\left( \sU\right) 	\bydef \Hom_L\left( \mathscr L\left(\sU \right), L \right). 
		$$
		\item[(ii)] There is a natural isomorphism of sheaves
		$$
		\mathfrak D	\mathfrak D \left( \mathscr L \right)\left( \sU\right) 	\bydef \Hom_L\left(\mathfrak D \mathscr L\left(\sU \right), L \right). 
		$$
		
		\item[(iii)] If $\sV, \sU \in \mathfrak{Closed}\left( \sX\right)$ such that $\sV \subset \sU$ and if $i^{^\neg\sV, ^\neg\sU}: \mathfrak D \left( \mathscr L \right)\left( ^\neg\sV\right)\to \mathfrak D \left( \mathscr L \right)\left(^\neg \sU\right)$ 
		then maps
		\bean
		^\neg  \mathscr L:	\mathfrak{Closed}\left( \sX\right)\to \mathbf{Ab},\quad 
		\sU \mapsto \mathfrak D \left( \mathscr L \right)\left(^\neg \sU \right);\\
		^\neg r^{\sV,\sU} : ^\neg  \mathscr L \left( \sU  \right)\to ^\neg  \mathscr L \left( \sV  \right)\quad \mathrm{equivalently}\quad \mathfrak D \left( \mathscr L \right)\left(^\neg \sU \right)\to \mathfrak D \left( \mathscr L \right)\left(^\neg \sV \right)
		\eean
		yield a sheaf on the locale $\mathfrak{Closed}\left( \sX\right)$.
	\end{enumerate}
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}(i)
	Since $\sX$ is compact any open subset $\sU$ is a finite disjoint union of connected components, i.e. $\sU = \bigsqcup_{j = 1}^n \sU_j$. It follows that $\mathscr L\left( \sU\right) =  L^n$ and $\mathfrak D \left( \mathscr L \right)\left( \sU\right) 	\bydef \Hom_L\left( L^n, L \right) \cong L^n$.
	The isomorphism \eqref{sh_co_eqn} is a simple consequence of these fact.\\
	(ii) Can be proven similarly (i).\\
	(iii) If $\sU, \sV \in\mathfrak{Closed}\left( \sX\right)$ such that $\sV \subset \sU$ then $^\neg \sU\subset ^\neg \sV$, and there is the natural map
	$$ !!!
	i_{\sV\sU}:\mathfrak A\left(^\neg \sV\ \right) \to \mathfrak A\left(^\neg \sU\ \right). 
	$$
	If $\left\{\sU_{   \a}\right\}_{\a \in \A}\subset \mathfrak{Closed}\left( \sX\right)$ then $\sU \bydef \bigvee \sU_\a$ is a closure of  $\bigcup \sU_\a$. So $^\neg \sU$ is a maximal open subset of $\bigcap ^\neg \sU_\a$.  Consider a family $\left\{s_\a \in \neg \mathscr L\left( \sU_\a\right) = \mathfrak D \left( \mathscr L \right)\left(^\neg \sU_\a \right) \right\}$ such that for all $\a_1, \a_2\in \A$ one has
	\bean
	i_{\neg \sU_{   \a_1};\ \neg ( \sU_{   \a_1}\cap ~\sU_{   \a_2}) }\left( s_{\a_1}\right) = i_{\neg \sU_{   \a_1};\ \neg ( \sU_{   \a_1}\cap ~\sU_{   \a_2}) }\left( s_{\a_2}\right) 
	\eean
	From the above equation and (i) it follows that 
	$$
	\varphi^{-1}_{\neg ( \sU_{   \a_1}\cap ~\sU_{   \a_2})}\left( i_{\neg \sU_{   \a_1};\ \neg ( \sU_{   \a_1}\cap ~\sU_{   \a_2}) }\left( s_{\a_1}\right)\right)=\varphi^{-1}_{\neg ( \sU_{   \a_1}\cap ~\sU_{   \a_2})}\left( i_{\neg \sU_{   \a_2};\ \neg ( \sU_{   \a_1}\cap ~\sU_{   \a_2}) }\left( s_{\a_2}\right)\right)
	$$
	where $\varphi_{\neg ( \sU_{   \a_1}\cap ~\sU_{   \a_2})}$ is given by \eqref{sh_co_eqn}. In particular one has
	\bean
	\left.\varphi^{-1}_{\neg ( \sU_{   \a_1}\cap ~\sU_{   \a_2})}\left( i_{\neg \sU_{   \a_1};\ \neg ( \sU_{   \a_1}\cap ~\sU_{   \a_2}) }\left( s_{\a_1}\right)\right)\right|_{\neg \sU }= \left.\varphi^{-1}_{\neg ( \sU_{   \a_1}\cap ~\sU_{   \a_2})}\left( i_{\neg \sU_{   \a_2};\ \neg ( \sU_{   \a_1}\cap ~\sU_{   \a_2}) }\left( s_{\a_2}\right)\right)\right|_{\neg\sU }
	\eean 
	So there is $t \in \neg \mathscr L\left(\sU \right)= $ 
	
	
	(cf. Equation \ref{sheaf_supp_eqn})
	On the other hand $\left|  \varphi^{-1}_{\sX}\left( \rho_{\sU_{   \a''},\sX}\left( s_{\a'}\right)\right)\right|\subset \overline \sU_\a$ where $\overline \sU_\a$ is the closure of $\sU_\a$. It turns out that there is.
	
\end{proof}

\begin{empt}
	Let $\mathbb{K}$ be a possible noncommutative field. Let $\mathscr P_{{\mathbb{K}}} \bydef {\mathbb{K}}_\sX  $ id a constant presheaf (cf. Definition \ref{constant_presheaf_defn}) on $\sX$, let $\mathscr A \bydef \mathfrak{Uss}\left(\mathscr P_{{\mathbb{K}}} \right)$ be an associated sheaf (cf. Definition \ref{associated_sheaf_defn}). 
	For any open  $\sU, \sV\subset \sX$ such that $\sV \subset \sU$ one has homomorphism
	\be\label{top_rv_eqn}
	\rho_{\sU \sV}: \mathscr A\left(\sU \right) \to \mathscr A\left(\sV \right),
	\\ \quad s \mapsto s|_\sV.
	\ee
	If $\sU\subset  \sX$ is an open subset and $\sU = \bigsqcup \sU_{\iota\in I}$ is a disjoint union of connected components then any $s \in A\left(\sU \right)$ corresponds to a family $\left\{s_\iota\right\}_{\iota\in I}\subset \mathbb{K}$.

	\sU\right) , \mathbb{K} \right)$ then any $t \in \mathfrak{A}\left(\sU \right)$ corresponds to a family $\left\{t_\iota\right\}_{\iota\in I}\subset \Hom_{\mathbb{K}}\left( \mathbb{K}, \mathbb{K}\right)$. 	\bean
	\mathfrak{A}\left(\sU \right) \cong \mathscr{A}\left(\sU \right)
	\eean
	If $\sU, \sV\subset \sX$ such that $\sV \subset \sU$ then there is the natural adjoint to \eqref{top_rv_eqn}
	\be\label{top_a_rv_eqn}
	\begin{split}
		\rho^*_{\sV \sU}: \mathfrak A\left(\sV \right)\cong \Hom_{\mathbb{K}}\left(\mathscr A\left(\sV \right) , \mathbb{K}\right)  \to \mathfrak A\left(\sU \right) \cong \Hom_{\mathbb{K}}\left(\mathscr A\left(\sU \right), \mathbb{K}\right).
	\end{split}
	\ee
	One can proof that $\mathfrak A$ is a cosheaf of $\mathbb{K}$-modules (cf. Definition \ref{top_cosh_defn}).
	Let
	$$
	\mathscr A = \mathscr A^0 \to \mathscr A^1\to \mathscr A^2\to \dots
	$$
	be a canonical injective resolution (cf. \ref{canonical_resolution_empt}) of $\mathscr A$ and for any $p \ge 0$ denote by $\mathfrak A_p$ a precosheaf
	$$
	\sU \mapsto  \Hom_{\mathbb{K}}\left(\mathscr A^p\left(\sU\right) , \mathbb{K} \right) 
	$$
\end{empt}
\begin{definition}\label{top_reg_defn}\cite{counter_topology}
	An open set is called \textit{regular open} if it is a maximal open subset of its closure. We say that a closed set is \textit{regular closed} if it is the closure of its maximal open subset.
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}
	Denition 1.1. Let X be a topological space. A family of contractible open
	subspaces Ui forms a good (open) cover if every nonempty intersection Ui1
	\
	: : : \ Uin is contractible.
\end{definition}
\begin{definition}
	Denition 2.4. If X is a finite CW complex, a good closed cover is a family of
	contractible subcomplexes ?Xi  such that every intersection of the Xi is either
	empty or contractible
\end{definition}
\begin{definition}
	By a closed covering a of a compact space X, we will always mean an
	ordered, finite covering a = (A1, ?  ? , Ar) by closed sets in X such that every
	point of X is in the interior of some A'.
\end{definition}
\begin{empt}
	Any cover of a manifold by geodesically convex open subsets is a good cover;
	by convexity, every nonempty intersection of them is contractible.
\end{empt}
	\subsection{Cosheaves}
\begin{definition}\label{top_res_defn}
	PAGE 34
	1.1. Definition. A \textit{graded sheaf} $\mathscr L^*$ is a sequence $\left\{\mathscr L^p\right\}$ of sheaves, $p$
	ranging over the integers. A \textit{differential sheaf} is a graded sheaf together
	with homomorphisms $d : \mathscr L^p \to \mathscr L^{p+1}$ such that $d^2: \mathscr L^p \to \mathscr L^{p+2}$ is zero for
	all $p$. A \textit{resolution} of a sheaf $d$ is a differential sheaf $\mathscr L^{p}$ with $\mathscr L_p = 0$
	for $p < 0$ together with an \textit{augmentation} homomorphism $\eps : \mathscr A \to \mathscr L^0$
	such that the sequence $0\to \mathscr A  \xrightarrow{\eps}\mathscr L^0  \xrightarrow{d}\mathscr L^{1}  \xrightarrow{d}\mathscr L^2\to \dots $ is exact.
\end{definition}


\paragraph{}
\begin{definition}\label{top_dim_defn}
	16.3. Definition. PAGE 111
	
	Let $\Phi$ be a family of supports on $\sX$ and let $\Phi$ be a fixed
	ground ring with unit. We let $\dim_{\Phi, L} \sX$ be the least integer $n$ (or $\infty$) such
	that  $H^{n+1}\left(\sX, \mathscr A \right)  = 0$ for all sheaves 4 of $L$-modules and all $k > n$.
\end{definition}
\begin{notation}  PAGE 69
	In \cite{bredon:sheaf} the following notation is used
	\be\label{top_ep_eqn}
	E\left(\Phi \right) \bydef \bigcup \left\{K | K \in \Phi\right\}.
	\ee
\end{notation}
\begin{definition}\label{top_ac_defn}\cite{bredon:sheaf}
	The sheaf $\mathscr A$ is
	said to be $\Phi$-acyclic if
	$$
	H^{p}\left( \sX, \mathscr A\right) = 0, \quad \forall p > 0.
	$$
\end{definition}
\begin{theorem}\label{top_c_ac_thm}
	PAGE 111	16.4. Theorem. The following four statements are equivalent:
	\begin{enumerate}
		\item[(a)] $\dim_{\Phi, L}\sX \le n$.
		\item[(b)] $H^{n+1}\left( \sX, \mathscr A\right) = 0$ for all sheaves $\mathscr A$ of $L$-modules.
		%	(c) For every sheaf .d of L-modules, Zn(X; 4) is D-acyclic.
		\item[(c)] Every sheaf $\mathscr A$ of $L$-modules has a $\Phi$-acyclic resolution of length $n$.
		
	\end{enumerate}	
	Moreover, if $\Phi$ is paracompactifying, then "$\Phi$-acyclic" in (c) 7 can	be replaced by "$\Phi$-soft."
\end{theorem}




\begin{definition}\label{top_loc_para_defn}\cite{bredon:sheaf}
	PAGE 112 We say that a space $\sX$ is locally paracompact  if it is Hausdorff and each
	point has a closed paracompact neighborhood. Such a space possesses a
	paracompactifying family $E\left(\Phi \right)=\sX$ (and conversely). Indeed, the
	set of all closed K such that K has a closed paracompact neighborhood in
	X is such a family.
\end{definition}
\begin{proposition}\label{top_loc_para_prop}
	PAGE 112 16.5. Proposition. If $\Phi$ and $\Psi$ are paracompactifying and $E\left(\Phi \right)\subset E\left(\Psi \right)$ ,
	then any $\Psi$-soft sheaf is $\Phi$-soft and $\dim_{\Phi, L} \sX \le \dim_{\Psi, L} \sX$ 
\end{proposition}
\begin{definition}\label{top_diml_defn}
	PAGE 112.
	$\sX$ is locally paracompact (cf. Definition \ref{top_loc_para_defn}) then we define (using \ref{top_loc_para_prop})
	$\dim_L\sX \bydef \dim_{\Phi, L}$, where $\Phi$ is paracompactifying and $E\left( \Phi\right)= \sX$.
\end{definition}


\begin{theorem}\label{top_loc_dim_thm}
	16.14. PAGE 115 Theorem. Let X be locally compact Hausdorff and L a ring with
	unit. Then $\dim_L \sX \le n \Leftrightarrow H^{n+1}\left(\sU, L \right)= 0$  for all open sets $\sU \subset \sX$.
\end{theorem}
\begin{proposition}
	PAGE 285
	1.10. Proposition. Let $\mathfrak A$ be a cosheaf and $M$ an $L$-module. Then the
	presheaf $\sU \mapsto \Hom\left(\mathfrak A (\sU), M \right)$ is a sheaf, denoted by  $\mathscr Hom\left(\mathfrak A, M \right)$ It is
	flabby if $\mathfrak A$ is flabby and $M$ is an injective module. It is torsion free if each
	$\mathfrak A(\sU)$ is divisible.
\end{proposition}

\begin{theorem}\label{top_free_l_thm}
	PAGE 314.   5.14. Theorem. Let $\sX$ be locally connected. If either $L$ is a field or $\sX$ is
	$clc^1_L$, then $H^c_0\left(\sX, L \right)$  is the free $L$-module on the components of $\sX$.
\end{theorem}
\begin{remark}\label{top_free_l_rem}
	The proof of the Theorem is based on the following fact
	\be\label{top_free_l_eqn}
	H^c_0 (\sX; L) \cong \varinjlim_{K}  H^c_0 (K; L)\cong \varinjlim_{K} \Hom\left( H^0\left(K ; L\right) , L\right) \cong  L.
	\ee
	where the set $\sX$ is connected and the sets $K$ are compact.
\end{remark}

	is an isomorphzsm.9
\end{theorem}
\begin{theorem}
	PAGE 309
	5.6\ Theorem. $\left(\mathscr M, \Phi \right)$  be elementary Then:
	\begin{enumerate}

		\item[	(ii)] The canonical map $H^\Phi_*\left(\sX, \mathscr M \right)$  is an isomorphism
		for any replete resolution $\mathcal L^*$ of $L$.
	\end{enumerate}

\end{theorem}
\begin{definition}\label{top_ndiml_defn}\cite{bredon:sheaf}
	PAGE 329 9.1. Definition. 
	
	A locally compact space $\sX$ is called an $n$-\textit{dimensional
		weak homology manifold over} $L$ (abbreviated as $n$-$whm_L$) if $\dim_L \sX < \infty$
	and $\mathscr H_p (\sX; L) = 0$ for $p\neq n$ and is torsion-free if $p=n$.  $\mathscr H_p (\sX; L) = 0$ is
	called the \textit{orientation sheaf} of $\sX$ and will be denoted by $\mathscr O = \mathscr O_\sX$. An
	$n$-$whm_L$ $\sX$ is said to be an $n$-\textit{dimensional homology manifold over} $L$
	(abbreviated as $n$-$hm_L$) if $\mathscr O$ is locally constant with stalks isomorphic to
	$L$. It is said to be \textit{orientable} if $\mathscr O$ is constant.
\end{definition}
\begin{remark}\label{top_dim_rem}
	PAGE 331
	It is proven in \cite{bredon:sheaf} that
	\be\label{top_dim_eqn}
	\dim_L \sX= n \text{ for an } n\text{-}hm_L \sX,
	\ee
	(cf. Definition \ref{top_diml_defn}).
\end{remark}
\begin{definition}
	PAGE 375
	16.7 Definition. The space $\sX$ is called an $n$-\textit{dimensional cohomology
		manifold over} $L$ (denoted $n$-$cm_L$) if $\sX$ has locally constant cohomology
	modules, locally equivalent to $L$ in degree $n$, and to zero in degrees other
	than $n$, and if $\dim_L\sX < \infty$.
\end{definition}
\begin{theorem}
	PAGE 375 16.8. Theorem. If $\sX$ is connected, then the following four conditions are
	equivalent:
	\begin{enumerate}
		\item 	[(a)] is an $n$-$cm_L$L.
		\item 		[(b)] $\dim_L \sX = n < \infty$ and $\sX$ has locally constant cohomology modules
		locally equivalent to $M^*$, when $M^*$ is finitely generated.
		\item 		[(c)] $\sX$ is $clc_L^\infty$ and  an $n$-$hm_L$.
		\item 		[(d)] $\sX$ is  $clc_L^\infty$, $\dim_L \sX < \infty$, and the stalks of $\mathscr H_r\left(\sX, L \right)$  are
		zero for $r \neq n$ and are isomorphic to $L$ for $r = n$.
	\end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

	Corollary.16 9  337 If L is the integers or afield, then a locally compact
	space X is an n-cmL X is clci , dimL X < oo, and
	HP(X, X - {x}; L).; L, for p = n,
	0, for p o n
	for allxEX.
\end{corollary}
\begin{theorem}
	16.16.  PAGE 380
	
	Theorem. Let $\sX$ be a connected $n$-$cm_L$, let $F\subsetneqq\sX$  be a proper
	closed subset, let $\sU \subset 
	\sX$ be any nonempty open subset, and let $\mathscr M$ be an
	elementary sheaf on $\sX$. Then:
	\begin{enumerate}
		\item [(a)] If $\mathscr M$ has stalks $L$, then $H_n\left( F, \mathscr M\right)= 0$  and $H_{n-1}\left( F, \mathscr M\right)$ as torsion free;
		\item[(b)] $H^n_c\left(\sU, \mathscr O \right)$  is the free $L$ -module on the components of $\sU$;
		\item[(c)] $H^n_c\left(F,\mathscr M \right)=0;$
		\item[(d)] $j^*_{\sX, \sU} : H^n_c\left(\sU,\mathscr M \right)\to H^n_c\left(\sX,\mathscr M \right)$ is surjective;
		\item[(e)]$j^*_{\sX, \sU} : H^n_c\left(\sU,\mathscr O \right)\to H^n_c\left(\sX,\mathscr O \right)$ is an isomorphism if $\sU$ is connected;
		\item[(f)] $\sX$ is orientable $\Leftrightarrow H^c_n\left( \sX, L\right) = L$.
	\end{enumerate}
	
\end{theorem}

\begin{empt}\label{top_taut_empt}\cite{spanier:at}
	For a pair (A,B) in a topological space X we define a neighborhood 
	((7, V) of (A,B) to be a pair in X such that U is a neighborhood of A and V is 
	a neighborhood of B. The family of all neighborhoods of (A,B) in X is directed 
	downward by inclusion. Hence 
	%HQ(U,V; G) | (U,V) 
	a neighborhood of (A,B)
	is a direct system, and we define 
	%H%A,B; G) = lirru {H(C7,V; G)} 
	where (U,V) varies over neighborhoods of (A,B) [or over the cofinal family of 
	open neighborhoods of (A,B)]. The restriction maps Hi(U,V; G) -> H(A,B; G) 
	define a natural homomorphism 
	%f: H<i{A,B; G) -> H(A,B; G) 
		The pair (A,B) is said to be 
		
		\textit{tautly imbedded} in X, or to be a taut pair in X 
		(with respect to singular cohomology), if i is an isomorphism for all q and G. 
		The definition of tautness can be formulated for any cohomology theory (or 
		any contravariant functor). We shall see examples later of a subspace taut 
		with respect to one cohomology theory but not with respect to another. 
	\end{empt}

		PAGE 126.

		$$

		for all $k$.
		X is said to be $clc_L$ if given $x$ and $N$ as above, $M$ can be chosen,

	\end{definition}

	\begin{definition} PAGE 350.
		12.4. Definition.
		A locally compact space $\sX$  is said to be $k$-$hlc_L$ if for
		each $x \in \sX$ and each neighborhood $\sU$ of $x$ there is a neighborhood $\sV \subset \sU$
		of $x$ such that the homomorphism
		$$
		i^{\sU, \sV}_* : \tilde H^c_k\left(\sV, L\right) \to \tilde H^c_k\left( \sU, L\right)
		$$
		is trivial. The space $\sX$ is $hlc^n_L$ if it is $k$-$hlcL$ for all $k \le n$.
	\end{definition}
	\begin{definition}\label{top_quasi_res}
		PAGE 350 12.5
		Definition. Let $M$ be an $L$-module. Let $\mathscr L^*$ be a differential sheaf
		and let $\eps: M \to H^0\left( \mathscr L^*\right)$  be a homomorphism of presheaves (i.e. $M \to 
		H^0\left( \mathscr L^*\left( 
		\sU\right) \right)$  for all open $\sU$). Then $\mathscr L^*$, together with $\eps$, is called a \textit{quasi}-$n$-\textit{resolution} of $M$ provided that
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item[(a)]  $\mathscr L^*$ is bounded below,
			\item[	(b)] $\mathscr H^p\left( \mathscr L^*\right)= 0$  for all $0 \neq p \le  n$,
			\item[	(c)] $\eps$ induces an isomorphism $M \to \mathscr H^0\left( \mathscr L^*\right)$  of sheaves.
		\end{enumerate}
		
		Similarly, let $\mathfrak{L}_*$ be a differential cosheaf and $\eta: H_0\left( \mathfrak L_*\right)  \to L$ a homomorphism
		of \ref{comm_alg_loc_thm}  !!! THEOREM !!!sheaves. Then $\mathfrak{L}_*$, together with $\eta$, is called a \textit{quasi}-$n$-\textit{coresolution}
		of $L$ provided that
		\begin{enumerate}
			\item [(a)] $\mathfrak{L}_*$ is bounded below,
			\item[	(b)] $H_p\left( \mathfrak{L}_*\right) =0$ for $p< 0$ and $H_p\left( \mathfrak{L}_*\right)$ is locally zero for $0\neq p \le n$
			\item[	(c)] $\eta$ is a local isomorphism of \ref{comm_alg_loc_thm}  !!! THEOREM !!!sheaves.
		\end{enumerate}
		
		We call $\mathfrak{L}_*$ an -$n$-\textit{coresolution} if also $\mathfrak{L}_p = 0$ for $p < 0$.
	\end{definition}
	\begin{theorem}
		PAGE 351
		12.6. Theorem. 
		$\sX$ is $hlc^n_L\Leftrightarrow C^c_*\left(\bullet, L \right)$  is a quasi-$n$-coresolution of $L$.
	\end{theorem}

	\end{theorem}
	\begin{theorem}\label{top_from_sh_to_cosh_thm}

		$\sX$ is $hlc_L^n$ and there as a natural isomorphism $H_p \left( \mathfrak D \left(\mathscr L^*\right)\right) \left(\sU \right) = H^c_p\left(\sU, L \right)$

	\begin{definition}\label{top_cosh_defn}\cite{bredon:sheaf}
		%1.1. Definition. 
		A \textit{precosheaf} on a topological space $\sX$ is a covariant functor from the
		category of open subsets of $\sX$ to that of $L$-modules. A precosheaf is a
		\textit{cosheaf} if the sequence
		\begin{equation}\label{topcoeq cosh}
			\bigoplus_{\bt, \ga\in \mathscr A} \mathfrak{A}(\sU_\bt \cap \sU_\ga) \xrightarrow{g} \bigoplus_{\a \in \mathscr A}  \mathfrak{A}(\sU_\a) \xrightarrow{f}  \mathfrak{A}(\sU)
		\end{equation}
		is exact for all collections $\left\{\sU_\a\right\}$ of open sets with $\sU = \cup_\a \sU_\a$,  where\\ $g \bydef \sum_{\bt, \ga} \left(i_{\sU_\bt, \sU_{\bt, \ga}} -i_{\sU_\ga, \sU_{\ga, \bt}}\right)$
		and $f\bydef \sum_\a i_{\sU, \sU_\a}$ ($i_{\sU,\sV}$ being the canonical
		homomorphism $\mathfrak{A}\left( \sV\right) \to \mathfrak{A}\left( \sU\right)$  for $\sV \to \sU$).
	\end{definition}

	\begin{definition}\label{sheaf_defn!}\cite{milne:lec}
		A \textit{sheaf} for the Grothendieck pretopology
		is a presheaf $\mathscr F$ 
		that satisfies the sheaf condition, that is a sequence 
		\be\label{sheaf_eqn!}
		\mathscr F \left(U\right) \to \prod_{\iota \in I} \mathscr F \left(U_\iota \right)\rightrightarrows  \prod_{\iota, j \in I\times I} \mathscr F \left(U_\iota \times_U U_j\right)
		\ee
		is exact for every covering $\left\{U_\iota \to U\right\}$. Thus $\mathscr F$ 
		is a sheaf if the map 
		$$
		f \mapsto \left\{f|_{U_\iota }\right\}:\mathscr F \left(U\right) \to \prod_{\iota \in I} \mathscr F \left(U_\iota \right)
		$$
		identifies $\mathscr F\left( U\right)$ with the subset of the product consisting of families $\left\{f|_{U_\iota }\right\}$ such that 
		$$
		f_\iota|_{U_\iota \times_U U_j}= f_j|_{U_\iota \times_U U_j}
		$$
		for all $\iota, j \in I$. 
		When $\mathbf T$ 
		is the site arising from a topological space then these definitions coincide with the 
		topological ones. 
		
	\end{definition}
	\paragraph{DELETE END}

	\end{corollary}

	\begin{definition}\label{top_const_cosh_defn}\cite{bredon:sheaf}  !!! COMPARE !!!
		The \textit{constant precosheaf} $L$ is the precosheaf taking the value $L$ on each
		$\sU$. There is no generally acceptable notion of a constant cosheaf on spaces
		that are not locally connected, but
		on locally connected spaces, a suitable notion of constant cosheaf is the
		precosheaf assigning to $\sU$ the free $L$-module on the components of $\sU$.
	\end{definition}
	\begin{definition}\label{top_flabby_cosh_defn}\cite{bredon:sheaf}
		%1.2. Definition. 
		A cosheaf $\mathfrak{A}$ is said to be \textit{flabby} if $\mathfrak{A}\left( \sU\right) \to\mathfrak{A}\left( \sX\right) $
		is a monomorphism for each open $\sU\subset\sX$.
	\end{definition}
	
	\begin{prop}
		%1.10. Proposition. 
		Let $\mathfrak{A}$ be a cosheaf and $M$ an $L$-module. Then the
		presheaf $~\sU \mapsto \Hom\left(\mathfrak{A}\left(\sU \right) , M \right)$  is a sheaf, denoted by $\mathscr Hom \left(\mathfrak{A} , M \right)$. It is
		flabby if $\mathfrak{A}$ is flabby and $M$ is an injective module. It is torsion free if each
		$\mathfrak{A}\left(\sU \right)$ is divisible.
	\end{prop}
	
	\begin{empt}
		Let $M$ be an $L$-module. $M$ has the canonical injective resolution $0 \hookto M \hookto M^0 \to M^1$ where $M^0 \bydef I(M)$ is the injective hull, and $M^1 \bydef I(M)/ M$. $M^1$ is
		injective since it is divisible and since $L$ is a principal ideal domain.
		
	\end{empt}
	\begin{definition}
		A \textit{differential cosheaf} $\mathfrak{A}_*$ is a graded cosheaf together with a differential
		$d : \mathfrak{A}_p \to \mathfrak{A}_{p -1}$ of degree -1 with $d^2 = 0$. For our purposes, $ \mathfrak{A}_p= 0$ will often
		vanish for $p > 0$.
	\end{definition}
	\begin{definition}
		The  \textit{dual} $\mathfrak{A}_*$ is a graded cosheaf together with a differential
		$d : \mathfrak{A}_p \to \mathfrak{A}_{p -1}$ of degree -1 with $d^2 = 0$. For our purposes, $ \mathfrak{A}_p= 0$ will often
		vanish for $p > 0$.
	\end{definition}
	\begin{definition}
		The 
		\textit{dual} of the differential cosheaf $\mathfrak{A}_*$, with respect to the $L$-module
		$M$ is defined to be the differential sheaf
		$$
		\mathscr D\left( \mathfrak{A}_*, M\right)\bydef \mathscr Hom \left(\mathfrak{A}_*, M^* \right)  
		$$
		where as usual, the term in degree $n$ is
		$$
		\mathscr D^n\left( \mathfrak{A}_*, M\right)= \bigoplus_{p + q = n} \mathscr Hom\left( \mathfrak{A}_p, M^q\right) = \mathscr Hom\left( \mathfrak{A}_n, M^0\right) \bigoplus \mathscr Hom\left( \mathfrak{A}_{n -1}, M^1\right)
		$$
		and where the differential $\mathscr D^n\to \mathscr D^{n + 1}$ is $d= d' - d''$, $d'$ being the homomorphism
		induced by the differential $M^q \to M^{q + 1}$ and $(-1)d''$ being that
		induced by $\mathfrak{A}_{p+1}\to \mathfrak{A}_{p}$. Explicitly, if
		$$
		f \in \mathscr D^n\left( \mathfrak{A}_*, M\right)\left(\sU \right) = \bigoplus_{p + q = n}\Hom\left( \mathfrak{A}_p, M^q\right)\left(\sU  \right) 
		$$
		and if $
		a \in \mathfrak{A}_*(\sU)
		$ then
		$$
		(df)(a) = d(f (a)) - (-1)^n f (da)
		$$.
		Since $M^*$ is injective, $\mathscr D\left( \bullet, M\right)$  is an exact functor of differential cosheaves.
	\end{definition}

	\begin{definition}\label{top_coh_man_defn}\cite{bredon:sheaf}
		375
		16.7. Definition. The space $\sX$ is called an $n$-\textit{dimensional cohomology
			manifold over} $L$ (denoted $n$-$cr_L$) if $\sX$ has locally constant cohomology
		modules, locally equivalent to $L$ in degree $n$, and to zero in degrees other
		than $n$, and if $\dim_L\sX < \infty$.
	\end{definition}

	Let $\mathscr U = \left\{\sU_\a\right\}_{\a \in I}$ be an open covering of a space $\sX$ indexed by a set
	$I$ and let $\mathscr A$ be a presheaf on $X$. Then an $n$-cochain c of it is a function
	defined on ordered $(n + 1)$-tuples $\left((\a_0, ... , \a_n\right)$ of members of $I$ such that
	$\sU_{\a_0,..., \a_n} \bydef \sU_{\a_0} \cap \dots \cap \sU_{\a_n}$ with value $c_{\a_0,..., \a_n}\in \mathscr A\left( sU_{\a_0,..., \a_n}\right)$ 
	These form a group denoted by $\check C\left(\mathscr U, \mathscr A \right)$. An open set $\sV$ of $\sX$ is covered
	by $\mathscr U \cap \sV \bydef \left\{\sU_\a\right\}_{\a \in I}$. Thus we have the cochain group $\check C\left(\mathscr U\cap \sV, \mathscr A \right)$,
	and the assignment $\sV \mapsto  \check C\left(\mathscr U\cap \sV, \mathscr A \right)$ gives a presheaf on $\sV$, and hence a
	sheaf
	$$
	\check{\mathscr C}^n \left(\mathscr \sV, \mathscr A \right)\bydef \mathfrak{Ass}\left(V \mapsto  \check C\left(\mathscr U\cap \sV, \mathscr A \right)\right)  
	$$
	Thus it makes sense to speak of the support $\left|c \right|$  of a cochain, i.e., $\left|c \right|\bydef \left|\th\left( c \right) \right|$ , where $\th :\check C\left(\mathscr U; \mathscr A \right)\to \Ga \left( \check {\mathscr C}\left(\mathscr U; \mathscr A \right)\right)$ This defines the cochain group
	$\check  C\left(\mathscr U; \mathscr A \right)$ for a family $\Phi$ of supports on $\sX$. The coboundary operator
	$$
	d c\left(\a_0, ..., \a_{n+1}\right)\bydef~ \sum_{\iota = 0}^{n+1}\left( -1\right)^\iota c\left(\a_0, ..., \hat \a_\iota, ..., \a_{n+1}\right)|_{\sU_{\a_0, ..., \a_{n+1}}}
	$$
	The above equation is a specialization of \eqref{cech_differential_eqn}.
\end{empt}

\begin{remark}\label{top_chech_rem}
The \v{C}ech cohomology over topological space is a specialization of the theory explained in the section \ref{cech_cohomology_sec}.
\end{remark}
\begin{remark}\label{top_chech_cof_rem}\cite{bredon:sheaf}
Now, the \v{C}ech cohomology groups are not altered by restriction to any
cofinal system of coverings. Therefore, if $\sX$ is compact, we can restrict the
coverings used to finite coverings.
\end{remark}

Let $\mathscr A$ be a presheaf on $\sX$ and $\mathscr U = \left\{\sU_\a\right\}_{\a \in I}$ an open covering of
$\sX$. We shall use $\sigma^n$ to denote an ordered $n$-simplex $\left( \a_0, ..., \a_n\right)$, of the
nerve $N\left(\mathscr U\right)$ of $\mathscr U$, and $\sU_{\sigma^n}$ to denote $\sU_{\a_0,..., \a_n} \bydef \sU_{\a_0} \cap \dots \cap \sU_{\a_n}$. Let $c \in \check{C}\left(\sX; \mathscr A \right)$. Then the support $\left|c \right|$  of $c$ is characterized by
\be\label{top_cech_supp_eqn}
x \notin c\Leftrightarrow \exists V_x \ni 0=c\left(\sigma^n \right)|_{\sU_{\sigma^n}\cap \sV_x} \in \mathscr A\left( \sV_x \cap \sU_{\sigma^n} \right) \quad \forall \sigma^n \in N\left(\mathscr U\right)
\ee
where $\sV_x$ is an open neighborhood of $x$.

\begin{theorem}
	PAGE 193 
	4.13. Theorem. Let $\mathscr A$ be a sheaf on $\sX$ and let $\mathscr U = \left\{\sU_\a\right\}_{\a \in I}$ an open covering  of $\sX$ having the property that $H^p\left( \sU_{\sigma^n};\mathscr A \right)= 0$  for $p > 0$ and
	all $\sigma^n \in N\left(\mathscr U \right)$ , all $n$. Then there is a canonical isomorphism
	\be
	H^*\left(\sX, \mathscr A \right) \cong \check H^*\left( \mathscr U; \mathscr A\right). 
	\ee
\end{theorem}
\begin{proof}
	Let $\mathscr A^q \bydef  \mathscr C^q\left(\sX,\mathscr A^* \right)$  and consider the complex $\check C^p\left( \mathscr U,\mathscr A^*\right)$  for $p$
	fixed. Since $\check C^p\left( \mathscr U,\bullet\right)$ is an exact functor of presheaves, we have
	\bean
	H^q\left(\check C^p\left( \mathscr U; \mathscr A^*\right)\right) = \check C^p\left( \mathscr U; H^q\left( \Ga \mathscr A^*|\bullet\right)\right)  = C^p\left( \mathscr U; H^q\left( \bullet, \mathscr  A^*\right)\right) = \\
	\begin{cases}
		\check C^p\left( \mathscr U; \mathscr A^*\right) & q = 0\\
		0 & q \neq 0
	\end{cases}
	\eean
	by the hypothesis that $H^q\left( \sU_{\sigma^n};\mathscr A \right)= 0$ for $q > 0$.
	Consider the diagram
	\newline
	\begin{tikzpicture}
		\matrix (m) [matrix of math nodes,row sep=3em,column sep=4em,minimum width=2em]
		{
			& 0  & 0 & 0 & 0 &	\\ 
			0 & \Ga\left(\mathscr A \right) 		& \Ga\left(\mathscr A^0 \right) & \Ga\left(\mathscr A^1 \right)  &  \Ga\left(\mathscr A^2 \right)& \dots\\
			0 & \check C^0\left(\mathscr U, \mathscr A \right) 		& \check C^0\left(\mathscr U, \mathscr A^0 \right) & \check C^0\left(\mathscr U, \mathscr A^1 \right)  &  \check C^0\left(\mathscr U, \mathscr A^2 \right)& \dots \\0 & \check C^1\left(\mathscr U, \mathscr A \right) 		& \check C^1\left(\mathscr U, \mathscr A^0 \right) & \check C^1\left(\mathscr U, \mathscr A^1 \right)  &  \check C^1\left(\mathscr U, \mathscr A^2 \right)& \dots\\0 & \check C^2\left(\mathscr U, \mathscr A \right) 		& \check C^2\left(\mathscr U, \mathscr A^0 \right) & \check C^2\left(\mathscr U, \mathscr A^1 \right)  &  \check C^2\left(\mathscr U, \mathscr A^2 \right)& \dots\\& \vdots  & \vdots & \vdots & \vdots &
			\\};
		\path[-stealth]
		(m-1-2) edge node [right]  {} (m-2-2)
		(m-1-3) edge node [right]  {} (m-2-3)
		(m-1-4) edge node [right]  {} (m-2-4)
		(m-1-5) edge node [right]  {} (m-2-5)
		
		(m-2-1) edge node [right]  {} (m-2-2)
		(m-2-2) edge node [right]  {} (m-2-3)
		(m-2-3) edge node [right]  {} (m-2-4)
		(m-2-4) edge node [right]  {} (m-2-5)
		(m-2-5) edge node [right]  {} (m-2-6)
		(m-2-2) edge node [right]  {} (m-3-2)
		(m-2-3) edge node [right]  {} (m-3-3)
		(m-2-4) edge node [right]  {} (m-3-4)
		(m-2-5) edge node [right]  {} (m-3-5)
		
		(m-2-2) edge node [right]  {} (m-3-2)
		(m-2-3) edge node [right]  {} (m-2-3)
		(m-2-4) edge node [right]  {} (m-3-4)
		(m-2-5) edge node [right]  {} (m-3-5)
		
		(m-3-1) edge node [right]  {} (m-3-2)
		(m-3-2) edge node [right]  {} (m-3-3)
		(m-3-3) edge node [right]  {} (m-3-4)
		(m-3-4) edge node [right]  {} (m-3-5)
		(m-3-5) edge node [right]  {} (m-3-6)
		
		(m-3-2) edge node [right]  {} (m-4-2)
		(m-3-3) edge node [right]  {} (m-4-3)
		(m-3-4) edge node [right]  {} (m-4-4)
		(m-3-5) edge node [right]  {} (m-4-5)
		
		(m-4-1) edge node [right]  {} (m-4-2)
		(m-4-2) edge node [right]  {} (m-4-3)
		(m-4-3) edge node [right]  {} (m-4-4)
		(m-4-4) edge node [right]  {} (m-4-5)
		(m-4-5) edge node [right]  {} (m-4-6)
		
		(m-4-2) edge node [right]  {} (m-5-2)
		(m-4-3) edge node [right]  {} (m-5-3)
		(m-4-4) edge node [right]  {} (m-5-4)
		(m-4-5) edge node [right]  {} (m-5-5)
		
		(m-5-1) edge node [right]  {} (m-5-2)
		(m-5-2) edge node [right]  {} (m-5-3)
		(m-5-3) edge node [right]  {} (m-5-4)
		(m-5-4) edge node [right]  {} (m-5-5)
		(m-5-5) edge node [right]  {} (m-5-6)
		
		(m-5-2) edge node [right]  {} (m-6-2)
		(m-5-3) edge node [right]  {} (m-6-3)
		(m-5-4) edge node [right]  {} (m-6-4)
		(m-5-5) edge node [right]  {} (m-6-5)
		;
	\end{tikzpicture}
	\\
	
	
	in which the rows are exact, excepting the first, as just shown and the
	columns are exact, excepting the first, by 4.10 and 4.9. A diagram chase
	shows that the homology of the first (nontrivial) row is isomorphic to that
	of the first column.
\end{proof}

\paragraph{TOPOLOGICAL CECH FINISH}
\paragraph{title}
\begin{theorem}\label{top_free_l_thm!!!}
	PAGE 314.   5.14. Theorem. Let $\sX$ be locally connected. If either $L$ is a field or $\sX$ is
	$clc^1_L$, then $H^c_0\left(\sX, L \right)$  is the free $L$-module on the components of $\sX$.
\end{theorem}
\paragraph{title}
\begin{lemma}\label{sh_co_lem} 
	. Suppose there is an inclusion $\sX \hookto\sY$.  If $^\neg \sU \bydef \sY \setminus \sU$ for all $\sU \in  \mathfrak{Closed }\left( \sX\right)$ and $\mathfrak A$ is a flabby cosheaf on $\sY$ (cf. Definitions \ref{top_cosh_defn} and \ref{top_flabby_cosh_defn} then the map
	\be\label{top_sheaf_eqn}
	\sU \mapsto \mathfrak A\left(^\neg \sU\right)
	\ee 
	is a sheaf over the locale $\mathfrak{Closed }\left( \sX\right)$ (cf. Definition \ref{sheaf_defn}). 
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
	If $\sU, \sV \in  \mathfrak{Closed }\left( \sX\right)$ are such that $\sV \le \sU$ then $^\neg \sU \subset ^\neg \sV$. From the Definition \ref{top_presheaf_defn} it follows that there is the natural homomorphism
	$$
	i_{^\neg \sV, ^\neg \sU}  :\mathfrak{A}\left( ^\neg \sU\right)\to \mathfrak{A}\left( ^\neg \sV\right).
	$$
	From this circumstance it follows that the map \eqref{top_sheaf_eqn} is a presheaf on $\mathfrak{Closed}\left( \sX\right)$ (cf. Definition \ref{presheaf_defn}), we denote it by 
	$^\neg \mathfrak A\in \mathbf{PreShAb}\left(\mathfrak{Closed}\left( \sX\right)  \right)$ (cf. Remark \ref{ab_uss_rem}).  Any $s \in ^\neg \mathfrak A\left( \sU\right) $ corresponds to $^\neg s \in  \mathfrak A\left(^\neg \sU\right) $. Let us prove that $^\neg \mathfrak A$ is a sheaf (cf. Definition \ref{sheaf_defn}). Let $\left\{s_\a\right\}_{\a\in \A}$ be a family such that $s_\a \in ^\neg \mathfrak A\left( \sU_\a\right)$ and $\sU_\a\in \mathfrak{Closed }\left( \sX\right)$ such that   
	\bean
	s_{\a'}|_{\sU_{   \a'}\wedge\sU_{   \a''}}=s_{\a''}|_{\sU_{   \a'}\wedge\sU_{   \a''}}
	\eean 
	for all  $\a', \a'' \in \A$. It turns out that 
	\be\label{top_co_eqn}
	_{~^\neg \sU_{   \a'},  ^\neg\sU_{   \a'}\cup  ^\neg\sU_{   \a''}}~ ^\neg s_{\a'} = i_{^\neg \sU_{   \a''},  ^\neg\sU_{   \a'}\cup  ^\neg\sU_{   \a''}}~ ^\neg s_{\a''} 
	\ee
	Taking into account that $\left| s_\a\right|$ is a closed subset  $\sU_{   \a''}$ from \eqref{top_co_eqn} it follows that for all $\a \in \A$ one has
	$\left| s_\a\right| \subset \sU$ where $\sU$ is a maximal open subset of $\bigcap^\neg \sU_{   \a}$ So there is $s \in \neg \mathfrak A\left( \sU\right)$ such that $i_{^\neg \sU   ,  ^\neg\sU_{   \a'}}\neg s= \neg s_\a$ for all $\a \in \A$. Thus $s$ is the required by the Definition \ref{sheaf_defn} section.
\end{proof}
\begin{empt}
	Let $\sY$ be a compact  $n$-\textit{dimensional homology manifold over} principal ideal domain $L$ (cf. Definition \ref{top_ndiml_defn}). One has $\dim_L \sY = n$ (cf. the equation \eqref{top_dim_eqn}, and the Definition \ref{top_diml_defn}). If $L_\sY\in \mathbf{PreAb}\left(\sY \right) $ is a constant presheaf and $\mathscr{L} \bydef \mathfrak{Ass}\left( L_\sY\right)\in \mathbf{ShAb}\left(\sY \right) $ is the associated sheaf  then there is the canonical injective resolution (cf. \ref{canonical_resolution_empt}). From the Theorem \ref{top_diml_thm} the resolution looks like
	\bean
	0\to \mathscr L \to \mathscr L^1 \to \dots \mathscr L^n \to 0
	\eean 
	Moreover the resolution is $c$-soft (cf. Theorem \ref{top_diml_thm}). From the Theorem \ref{top_from_sh_to_cosh_thm} it follows that there is $\mathfrak D \left(\mathscr L^*\right)$  is a flabby quasi $n$-
	coresolution $\mathfrak D \left(\mathscr L^*\right)$ 
	\be
	0\to \mathfrak A \leftarrow \mathfrak A_1 \leftarrow \dots \leftarrow\mathfrak A_n 
	\ee
	
	
	
	from the the Theorem \ref{top_diml_thm} it follows that 
\end{empt}
